<li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_Buddhism" title="Outline of Buddhism">Outline</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_religion_world.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/31px-P_religion_world.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/47px-P_religion_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/62px-P_religion_world.svg.png 2x" width="31"/></a>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Religion" title="Portal:Religion">Religion portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Buddhism" title="Template:Buddhism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Buddhism" title="Template talk:Buddhism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Buddhism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>TheravÄda</b></i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ËŒ/: secondary stress follows">ËŒ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/É›r/: 'err' in 'merry'">É›r</span><span title="/É™/: 'a' in 'about'">É™</span><span title="/Ëˆ/: primary stress follows">Ëˆ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/É‘Ë/: 'a' in 'father'">É‘Ë</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/É™/: 'a' in 'about'">É™</span></span>/</a></span></span>) (<a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a>: <span lang="si">à¶®à·šà¶»à·€à·à¶¯à¶º</span>, <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>: <span lang="my">á€‘á€±á€›á€á€«á€’</span>, <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">à¹€à¸–à¸£à¸§à¸²à¸—</span>, <a href="/wiki/Khmer_language" title="Khmer language">Khmer</a>: <span lang="km">ááŸášáœá¶á‘</span>, <a href="/wiki/Lao_language" title="Lao language">Lao</a>: <span lang="lo">à»€àº–àº£àº°àº§àº²àº”</span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_language" title="Pali language">Pali</a>: <span lang="pi"><span title="Sanskrit-language text"><span lang="sa">ğ‘€£ğ‘‚ğ‘€­ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤</span></span></span>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">â€‰</span>'School of the Elders'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodhi_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodhi-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup>) is the most commonly accepted name of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>'s oldest existing school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodhi_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bodhi-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> The school's adherents, termed TheravÄdins, have preserved their version of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>'s teaching or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharma_(Buddhism)" title="Dharma (Buddhism)">Buddha Dhamma</a></i> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li_Canon" title="PÄli Canon">PÄli Canon</a> for over two millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodhi_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bodhi-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun2014_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun2014-3">[web 1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The PÄli Canon is the most complete Buddhist canon surviving in a <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">classical Indian language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">PÄli</a>, which serves as the school's <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">sacred language</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosby,_Kate_2013_p._2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby,_Kate_2013_p._2-4">[3]</a></sup> In contrast to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81y%C4%81na" title="MahÄyÄna">MahÄyÄna</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vajray%C4%81na" title="VajrayÄna">VajrayÄna</a></i>, TheravÄda tends to be conservative in matters of doctrine (<i><a href="/wiki/Pariyatti" title="Pariyatti">pariyatti</a></i>) and monastic discipline (<i><a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">vinaya</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> One element of this conservatism is the fact that TheravÄda rejects the authenticity of the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_sutras" title="Mahayana sutras">Mahayana sutras</a> (which appeared c. 1st century BCE onwards).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern TheravÄda derives from the <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura_Maha_Viharaya" title="Anuradhapura Maha Viharaya">MahÄvihÄra</a> order, a Sri Lankan branch of the <a href="/wiki/Vibhajyav%C4%81da" title="VibhajyavÄda">VibhajjavÄda</a> tradition, which is, in turn, a sect of the Indian <a href="/wiki/Sthavira_nik%C4%81ya" title="Sthavira nikÄya">Sthavira Nikaya</a>. This tradition began to establish itself in Sri Lanka from the 3rd century BCE onwards. It was in Sri Lanka that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li_Canon" title="PÄli Canon">PÄli Canon</a> was written down and the school's <a href="/wiki/Atthakatha" title="Atthakatha">commentary</a> literature developed. From Sri Lanka, the TheravÄda <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura_Maha_Viharaya" title="Anuradhapura Maha Viharaya">MahÄvihÄra</a> tradition subsequently <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Southeast_Asia" title="Buddhism in Southeast Asia">spread to the rest of Southeast Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> It is the official religion of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, and the dominant religion in <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and is practiced by minorities in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a> of all of these groups, as well as converts around the world, also embrace and practice TheravÄda Buddhism.
</p><p>During the modern era, new developments have included <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">Vipassana movement</a> which reinvigorated TheravÄda meditation practice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun2014_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Braun2014-3">[web 1]</a></sup> the growth of the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Forest_Tradition" title="Thai Forest Tradition">Thai Forest Tradition</a> which reemphasized forest monasticism and the spread of TheravÄda westward to places such as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, along with Buddhist immigrants and converts in the European Union and the United States.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Therav%C4%81da_Buddhism" title="History of TheravÄda Buddhism">History of TheravÄda Buddhism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Modern">Pre-Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg/240px-Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg/360px-Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg/480px-Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thuparamaya_Stupa_and_Stone_Pillars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Thuparamaya" title="Thuparamaya">Thuparamaya</a> Stupa, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a> after Theravada Buddhism became the official religion in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, dating back to the reign of King Devanampiya Tissa (247â€“207 BCE).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg/240px-Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg/360px-Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg/480px-Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ruwanweli_Saya_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ruwanwelisaya" title="Ruwanwelisaya">Ruwanwelisaya</a> stupa, built by the Sri Lankan <a href="/wiki/Dutugamunu" title="Dutugamunu">King Dutugemunu</a> (c. 140 B.C.E.).</div></div></div>
<p>The TheravÄda school descends from the <a href="/wiki/Vibhajyav%C4%81da" title="VibhajyavÄda">VibhajjavÄda</a>, a division within the <a href="/wiki/Sthavira_nik%C4%81ya" title="Sthavira nikÄya">SthÄvira nikÄya</a>, one of the two major orders that arose after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Buddhist_council" title="Second Buddhist council">first schism</a> in the Indian Buddhist community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cousins2001_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cousins2001-10">[9]</a></sup> TheravÄda sources trace their tradition to the Third Buddhist council when elder <a href="/wiki/Moggaliputta-Tissa" title="Moggaliputta-Tissa">Moggaliputta-Tissa</a> is said to have compiled the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kathavatthu" title="Kathavatthu">Kathavatthu</a></i>, an important work which lays out the VibhajjavÄda doctrinal position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berkwitz2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berkwitz2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aided by the patronage of Mauryan kings like <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>, this school spread throughout India and reached <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> through the efforts of missionary monks like <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a>. In Sri Lanka, it became known as the <a href="/wiki/Tamrashatiya" title="Tamrashatiya">Tambapaá¹‡á¹‡iya (and later as MahÄvihÄravÄsins)</a> which was based at the <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura_Maha_Viharaya" title="Anuradhapura Maha Viharaya">Great Vihara (Mahavihara)</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura" title="Anuradhapura">Anuradhapura</a> (the ancient Sri Lankan capital).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[11]</a></sup> According to TheravÄda sources, another one of the Ashokan missions was also sent to <a href="/wiki/Suvarnabhumi" title="Suvarnabhumi"><i>Suvaá¹‡á¹‡abhÅ«mi</i></a> ("The Golden Land"), which may refer to Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the first century BCE, TheravÄda Buddhism was well established in the main settlements of the <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura_Kingdom" title="Anuradhapura Kingdom">Kingdom of Anuradhapura</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> The Pali Canon, which contains the main scriptures of the TheravÄda, was committed to writing in the first century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> Throughout the history of ancient and medieval Sri Lanka, TheravÄda was the main religion of the <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_people" title="Sinhalese people">Sinhalese people</a> and its temples and monasteries were patronized by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sri_Lankan_monarchs" title="List of Sri Lankan monarchs">Sri Lankan kings</a>, who saw themselves as the protectors of the religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="1182" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg/240px-TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg/360px-TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg/480px-TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TipitakaGoldPlates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold Plates containing fragments of the Pali Tipitaka (5th century) found in Maunggan (a village near the city of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Ksetra_Kingdom" title="Sri Ksetra Kingdom">Sriksetra</a>).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_Bagan,_Myanmar,_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2248" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Old_Bagan%2C_Myanmar%2C_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg/240px-Old_Bagan%2C_Myanmar%2C_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Old_Bagan%2C_Myanmar%2C_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg/360px-Old_Bagan%2C_Myanmar%2C_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Old_Bagan%2C_Myanmar%2C_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg/480px-Old_Bagan%2C_Myanmar%2C_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_Bagan,_Myanmar,_Bagan_plains_at_sunset.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bagan" title="Bagan">Bagan</a>, the capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bagan_Kingdom" title="Bagan Kingdom">Bagan Kingdom</a>. Between the 11th and 13th centuries, more than 10,000 temples, <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagodas</a> and monasteries were constructed in the Bagan plains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Over time, two other sects split off from the MahÄvihÄra tradition, the <a href="/wiki/Abhayagiri_vih%C4%81ra" title="Abhayagiri vihÄra">Abhayagiri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jetavanaramaya" title="Jetavanaramaya">Jetavana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warder,_A.K._2000._p._280_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warder,_A.K._2000._p._280-18">[17]</a></sup> While the Abhayagiri sect became known for the <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> study of <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Vajrayana</a> texts, as well as the TheravÄda canon, the MahÄvihÄra tradition, did not accept these new scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Instead, MahÄvihÄra scholars like <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a> focused on the exegesis of the Pali scriptures and on the <a href="/wiki/Abhidharma" title="Abhidharma">Abhidhamma</a>. These TheravÄda sub-sects often came into conflict with each other over royal patronage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> The reign of <a href="/wiki/Parakramabahu_I" title="Parakramabahu I">ParÄkramabÄhu I</a> (1153â€“1186) saw an extensive reform of the Sri Lankan sangha after years of warfare on the island. ParÄkramabÄhu created a single unified sangha which came to be dominated by the MahÄvihÄra sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Epigraphical evidence has established that TheravÄda Buddhism became a dominant religion in the Southeast Asian kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Ksetra_Kingdom" title="Sri Ksetra Kingdom">Sri Ksetra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dvaravati" title="Dvaravati">Dvaravati</a> from about the 5th century CE onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> The oldest surviving Buddhist texts in the PÄli language are gold plates found at Sri Ksetra dated circa the 5th to 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Before the TheravÄda tradition became the dominant religion in Southeast Asia, MahÄyÄna, Vajrayana and Hinduism were also prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sujato,_Bhikkhu_2006._p._72_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sujato,_Bhikkhu_2006._p._72-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting at around the 11th century, Sinhalese TheravÄda monks and Southeast Asian elites led a widespread conversion of most of mainland Southeast Asia to the TheravÄdin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahaviharavasin" title="Mahaviharavasin">Mahavihara</a> school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> The patronage of monarchs such as the Burmese king <a href="/wiki/Anawrahta" title="Anawrahta">Anawrahta</a> (Pali: Aniruddha, 1044â€“1077) and the Thai king <a href="/wiki/Ram_Khamhaeng" title="Ram Khamhaeng">Ram Khamhaeng</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">floruit</a>. late 13th century) was instrumental in the rise of TheravÄda Buddhism as the predominant religion of Burma and Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:13-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Burmese and Thai kings saw themselves as <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_kingship" title="Buddhist kingship">Dhamma Kings</a> and as protectors of the TheravÄda faith. They promoted the building of new temples, patronized scholarship, monastic ordinations and missionary works as well as attempted to eliminate certain non-Buddhist practices like animal sacrifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geh-172-173_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-geh-172-173-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> During the 15th and 16th centuries, TheravÄda also became established as the state religion in Cambodia and Laos. In Cambodia, numerous Hindu and Mahayana temples, most famously <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Thom" title="Angkor Thom">Angkor Thom</a>, were transformed into TheravÄdin monasteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg/240px-Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg/360px-Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg/480px-Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhism-meditation-burma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Burmese man meditates in <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>. The widespread practice of meditation by laypersons is a modern development in TheravÄda.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th and 20th centuries, TheravÄda Buddhists came into direct contact with western ideologies, religions and modern science. The various responses to this encounter have been called "<a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> In the British colonies of <a href="/wiki/British_Ceylon" title="British Ceylon">Ceylon</a> (modern Sri Lanka) and <a href="/wiki/British_rule_in_Burma" title="British rule in Burma">Burma</a> (Myanmar), Buddhist institutions lost their traditional role as the prime providers of education (a role that was often filled by Christian schools).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> In response to this, Buddhist organizations were founded which sought to preserve Buddhist scholarship and provide a Buddhist education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anagarika_Dhammapala" title="Anagarika Dhammapala">Anagarika Dhammapala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Migettuwatte_Gunananda_Thera" title="Migettuwatte Gunananda Thera">Migettuwatte Gunananda Thera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hikkaduwe_Sri_Sumangala_Thera" title="Hikkaduwe Sri Sumangala Thera">Hikkaduwe Sri Sumangala Thera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Steel_Olcott" title="Henry Steel Olcott">Henry Steel Olcott</a> (one of the first American western converts to Buddhism) were some of the main figures of the Sri Lankan Buddhist revival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> Two new monastic orders were formed in the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Amarapura_Nik%C4%81ya" title="Amarapura NikÄya">Amarapura NikÄya</a> and the <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81ma%C3%B1%C3%B1a_Nik%C4%81ya" title="RÄmaÃ±Ã±a NikÄya">RÄmaÃ±Ã±a NikÄya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Burma, an influential modernist figure was king <a href="/wiki/Mindon_Min" title="Mindon Min">Mindon Min</a> (1808â€“1878), known for his patronage of the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Buddhist_council" title="Fifth Buddhist council">Fifth Buddhist council</a> (1871) and the <a href="/wiki/Tripi%E1%B9%ADaka_tablets_at_Kuthodaw_Pagoda" title="Tripiá¹­aka tablets at Kuthodaw Pagoda">Tripiá¹­aka tablets at Kuthodaw Pagoda</a> (still the world's largest book) with the intention of preserving the Buddha Dhamma. Burma also saw the growth of the "<a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">Vipassana movement</a>", which focused on reviving Buddhist meditation and <a href="/wiki/Pariyatti" title="Pariyatti">doctrinal learning</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ledi_Sayadaw" title="Ledi Sayadaw">Ledi Sayadaw</a> (1846â€“1923) was one of the key figures in this movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insight_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-insight-41">[40]</a></sup> After independence, Myanmar held the <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Buddhist_council" title="Sixth Buddhist council">Sixth Buddhist council</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vesak" title="Vesak">Vesak</a> 1954 to Vesak 1956) to create a new redaction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li_Canon" title="PÄli Canon">PÄli Canon</a>, which was then published by the government in 40 volumes. The Vipassana movement continued to grow after independence, becoming an international movement with centers around the world. Influential meditation teachers of the post-independence era include <a href="/wiki/U_N%C4%81rada" title="U NÄrada">U Narada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahasi_Sayadaw" title="Mahasi Sayadaw">Mahasi Sayadaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/U_Pandita" title="U Pandita">Sayadaw U Pandita</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyanaponika_Thera" title="Nyanaponika Thera">Nyanaponika Thera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Webu_Sayadaw" title="Webu Sayadaw">Webu Sayadaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sayagyi_U_Ba_Khin" title="Sayagyi U Ba Khin">U Ba Khin</a> and his student <a href="/wiki/S._N._Goenka" title="S. N. Goenka">S.N. Goenka.</a>
</p><p>Meanwhile, in Thailand (the only TheravÄda nation to retain its independence throughout the colonial era), the religion became much more centralized, <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucratized</a> and controlled by the state after a series of reforms promoted by Thai kings of the <a href="/wiki/Chakri_dynasty" title="Chakri dynasty">Chakri dynasty</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Mongkut" title="Mongkut">Mongkut</a> (r. 1851â€“1868) and his successor <a href="/wiki/Chulalongkorn" title="Chulalongkorn">Chulalongkorn</a> (1868â€“1910) were especially involved in centralizing sangha reforms. Under these kings, the sangha was organized into a hierarchical bureaucracy led by the <a href="/wiki/Sangha_Supreme_Council" title="Sangha Supreme Council">Sangha Council of Elders</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_language" title="Pali language">Pali</a>: <i>MahÄthera SamÄgama</i>), the highest body of the Thai sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:14-42">[41]</a></sup> Mongkut also led the creation of a new monastic order, the <a href="/wiki/Dhammayuttika_Nikaya" title="Dhammayuttika Nikaya">Dhammayuttika</a> Nikaya, which kept a stricter monastic discipline than the rest of the Thai sangha (this included not using money, not storing up food and not taking milk in the evening).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> The Dhammayuttika movement was characterized by an emphasis on the original Pali Canon and a rejection of Thai folk beliefs which were seen as irrational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:18-45">[44]</a></sup> Under the leadership of Prince <a href="/wiki/Vajirananavarorasa" title="Vajirananavarorasa">Wachirayan Warorot</a>, a new education and examination system was introduced for Thai monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:15-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AjahnChahSangha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AjahnChahSangha.jpg/240px-AjahnChahSangha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AjahnChahSangha.jpg/360px-AjahnChahSangha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AjahnChahSangha.jpg/480px-AjahnChahSangha.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AjahnChahSangha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thai Forest teacher <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Chah" title="Ajahn Chah">Ajahn Chah</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Sumedho" title="Ajahn Sumedho">Ajahn Sumedho</a> (front right), <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Pasanno" title="Ajahn Pasanno">Ajahn Pasanno</a> (rear and left of Sumedho) and other monastics.</div></div></div>
<p>The 20th century also saw the growth of "forest traditions" which focused on forest living and strict monastic discipline. The main forest movements of this era are the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Forest_Tradition" title="Sri Lankan Forest Tradition">Sri Lankan Forest Tradition</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Forest_Tradition" title="Thai Forest Tradition">Thai Forest Tradition</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Mun" title="Ajahn Mun">Ajahn Mun</a> (1870â€“1949) and his students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>TheravÄda Buddhism in Cambodia and Laos went through similar experiences in the modern era. Both had to endure French colonialism, destructive civil wars and oppressive communist governments. Under <a href="/wiki/French_protectorate_of_Cambodia" title="French protectorate of Cambodia">French Rule</a>, French indologists of the <a href="/wiki/French_School_of_the_Far_East" title="French School of the Far East">Ã‰cole franÃ§aise d'ExtrÃªme-Orient</a> became involved in the reform of Buddhism, setting up institutions for the training of Cambodian and Lao monks, such as the Ecole de Pali which was founded in Phnom Penh in 1914<i>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> While the Khmer Rouge effectively destroyed Cambodia's Buddhist institutions, after the end of the communist regime the Cambodian Sangha was re-established by monks who had returned from exile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_2001_p=75_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_2001_p=75-49">[48]</a></sup> In contrast, communist rule in Laos was less destructive since the <a href="/wiki/Pathet_Lao" title="Pathet Lao">Pathet Lao</a> sought to make use of the sangha for political ends by imposing direct state control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> During the late 1980s and 1990s, the official attitudes toward Buddhism began to liberalise in Laos and there was a resurgence of traditional Buddhist activities such as merit-making and doctrinal study.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Pagoda,Gorai,Meera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_(4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3005" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Global_Pagoda%2CGorai%2CMeera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_%284%29.jpg/240px-Global_Pagoda%2CGorai%2CMeera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_%284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Global_Pagoda%2CGorai%2CMeera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_%284%29.jpg/360px-Global_Pagoda%2CGorai%2CMeera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Global_Pagoda%2CGorai%2CMeera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_%284%29.jpg/480px-Global_Pagoda%2CGorai%2CMeera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_%284%29.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Pagoda,Gorai,Meera_Rd-Bhayandar_-_panoramio_(4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Global_Vipassana_Pagoda" title="Global Vipassana Pagoda">Global Vipassana Pagoda</a>, Maharashtra, India. S.N. Goenka laid the foundation for the structure in 2000 and the pagoda opened in 2009. Regular meditation courses are held at the complex.</div></div></div>
<p>The modern era also saw the spread of TheravÄda Buddhism around the world and the revival of the religion in places where it remains a minority faith. Some of the major events of the spread of modern TheravÄda include:
</p>
<ul><li>The 20th-century Nepalese TheravÄda movement which introduced TheravÄda Buddhism to <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and was led by prominent figures such as <a href="/wiki/Dharmaditya_Dharmacharya" title="Dharmaditya Dharmacharya">Dharmaditya Dharmacharya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bauddha_Rishi_Mahapragya" title="Bauddha Rishi Mahapragya">Mahapragya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pragyananda_Mahasthavir" title="Pragyananda Mahasthavir">Pragyananda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dhammalok_Mahasthavir" title="Dhammalok Mahasthavir">Dhammalok Mahasthavir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li>The establishment of some of the first TheravÄda Viharas in the Western world, such as the <a href="/wiki/London_Buddhist_Vihara" title="London Buddhist Vihara">London Buddhist Vihara</a> (1926), <a href="/wiki/Das_Buddhistische_Haus" title="Das Buddhistische Haus">Das Buddhistische Haus</a> in Berlin (1957) and the Washington Buddhist Vihara in Washington, DC (1965).</li>
<li>The founding of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Buddhist_Association" title="Bengal Buddhist Association">Bengal Buddhist Association</a> (1892) and the Dharmankur Vihar (1900) in <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Calcutta</a> by the Bengali monk <a href="/wiki/Kripasaran" title="Kripasaran">Kripasaran</a> Mahasthavir, which were key events in the Bengali TheravÄda revival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>The founding of the <a href="/wiki/Maha_Bodhi_Society" title="Maha Bodhi Society">Maha Bodhi Society</a> in 1891 by <a href="/wiki/Anagarika_Dharmapala" title="Anagarika Dharmapala">Anagarika Dharmapala</a> which focused on the conservation and restoration of important Indian Buddhist sites, such as <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Sarnath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jerryson,_Michael_K._p._41_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jerryson,_Michael_K._p._41-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahir_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahir-54">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>The introduction of TheravÄda to other Southeast Asian nations like Singapore, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>. Especially with Ven. <a href="/wiki/K._Sri_Dhammananda" title="K. Sri Dhammananda">K. Sri Dhammananda</a> missionary efforts among English-speaking Chinese communities.</li>
<li>The return of Western TheravÄdin monks trained in the Thai Forest Tradition to western countries and the subsequent founding of monasteries led by western monastics, such as <a href="/wiki/Abhayagiri_Buddhist_Monastery" title="Abhayagiri Buddhist Monastery">Abhayagiri Buddhist Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chithurst_Buddhist_Monastery" title="Chithurst Buddhist Monastery">Chithurst Buddhist Monastery</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metta_Forest_Monastery" title="Metta Forest Monastery">Metta Forest Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amaravati_Buddhist_Monastery" title="Amaravati Buddhist Monastery">Amaravati Buddhist Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birken_Forest_Buddhist_Monastery" title="Birken Forest Buddhist Monastery">Birken Forest Buddhist Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bodhinyana_Monastery" title="Bodhinyana Monastery">Bodhinyana Monastery</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santacittarama" title="Santacittarama">Santacittarama</a>.</li>
<li>The spread of the <a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">Vipassana movement</a> around the world by the efforts of people like <a href="/wiki/S._N._Goenka" title="S. N. Goenka">S.N. Goenka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anagarika_Munindra" title="Anagarika Munindra">Anagarika Munindra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goldstein_(writer)" title="Joseph Goldstein (writer)">Joseph Goldstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kornfield" title="Jack Kornfield">Jack Kornfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharon_Salzberg" title="Sharon Salzberg">Sharon Salzberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dipa_Ma" title="Dipa Ma">Dipa Ma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Denison" title="Ruth Denison">Ruth Denison</a>.</li>
<li>The Vietnamese TheravÄda movement, led by figures such as Ven. Há»™-TÃ´ng (Vansarakkhita).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Texts">Texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="P.C4.81li_Tipi.E1.B9.ADaka"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="PÄli_Tipiá¹­aka">PÄli Tipiá¹­aka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: PÄli Tipiá¹­aka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_(inside)_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2652" data-file-width="4254" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_%28inside%29_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg/240px-Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_%28inside%29_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_%28inside%29_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg/360px-Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_%28inside%29_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_%28inside%29_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg/480px-Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_%28inside%29_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustrated_Sinhalese_covers_(inside)_showing_the_events_Wellcome_L0031774.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pre-modern copies of the Tipiá¹­aka were preserved in <a href="/wiki/Palm-leaf_manuscript" title="Palm-leaf manuscript">Palm-leaf manuscripts</a>, most of which have not survived the humid climate of South Asia and Southeast Asia.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tipitaka1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1733" data-file-width="1320" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tipitaka1.jpg/240px-Tipitaka1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tipitaka1.jpg/360px-Tipitaka1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tipitaka1.jpg/480px-Tipitaka1.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tipitaka1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A full modern set of the Tipiá¹­aka can fill many volumes (from 40 to over 50 volumes depending on the edition).</div></div></div>
<p>According to Kate Crosby, for TheravÄda, the PÄli <a href="/wiki/Tripi%E1%B9%ADaka" title="Tripiá¹­aka">Tipiá¹­aka</a>, also known as the PÄli Canon is "the highest authority on what constitutes the Dhamma (the truth or teaching of the Buddha) and the organization of the Sangha (the community of monks and nuns)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The language of the Tipiá¹­aka, <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">PÄli</a>, is a <a href="/wiki/Middle_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Middle Indo-Aryan languages">middle-Indic language</a> which is the main religious and scholarly language in TheravÄda. This language may have evolved out of various Indian dialects, and is related to, but not the same as, the ancient language of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>An early form of the Tipiá¹­aka may have been transmitted to <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>, which saw a period of Buddhist missionary activity. After being orally transmitted (as was the custom for religious texts in those days) for some centuries, the texts were finally committed to writing in the 1st century BCE. TheravÄda is one of the first Buddhist schools to commit its Tipiá¹­aka to writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Recension" title="Recension">recension</a> of the Tipiá¹­aka which survives today is that of the Sri Lankan Mahavihara sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest manuscripts of the Tipiá¹­aka from Sri Lanka and Southeast Asia date to the 15th Century, and they are incomplete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skilling_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-skilling-60">[59]</a></sup> Complete manuscripts of the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikaya" title="Nikaya">Nikayas</a> are only available from the 17th Century onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-analayo_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-analayo-61">[60]</a></sup> However, fragments of the Tipiá¹­aka have been found in inscriptions from Southeast Asia, the earliest of which have been dated to the 3rd or 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skilling_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-skilling-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0h_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0h-62">[61]</a></sup> According to Alexander Wynne, "they agree almost exactly with extant PÄli manuscripts. This means that the PÄli Tipiá¹­aka has been transmitted with a high degree of accuracy for well over 1,500 years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0h_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0h-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are numerous editions of the Tipiá¹­aka, some of the major modern editions include the <a href="/wiki/Pali_Text_Society" title="Pali Text Society">Pali Text Society</a> edition (published in Roman script), the Burmese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_Councils" title="Buddhist Councils">Sixth Council</a> edition (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burmese_script" title="Burmese script">Burmese script</a>, 1954â€“56) and the Thai Tipiá¹­aka edited and published in <a href="/wiki/Thai_script" title="Thai script">Thai script</a> after the council held during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Prajadhipok" title="Prajadhipok">Rama VII</a> (1925â€“35). There is also a <a href="/wiki/Khmer_script" title="Khmer script">Khmer</a> edition, published in <a href="/wiki/Phnom_Penh" title="Phnom Penh">Phnom Penh</a> (1931â€“69).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The PÄli Tipitaka consists of three parts: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vinaya_Pitaka" title="Vinaya Pitaka">Vinaya Pitaka</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sutta_Pitaka" title="Sutta Pitaka">Sutta Pitaka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abhidhamma_Pitaka" title="Abhidhamma Pitaka">Abhidhamma Pitaka</a>. Of these, the Abhidhamma Pitaka is believed to be a later addition to the collection, its composition dating from around the 3rd century BCE onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> The PÄli Abhidhamma was not recognized outside the TheravÄda school. There are also some texts which were late additions that are included in the fifth Nikaya, the <i><a href="/wiki/Khuddaka_Nik%C4%81ya" title="Khuddaka NikÄya">Khuddaka NikÄya</a></i> ('Minor Collection'), such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pa%E1%B9%ADisambhid%C4%81magga" title="Paá¹­isambhidÄmagga">Paá¹­isambhidÄmagga</a></i> (possibly c. 3rd to 1st century BCE) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Buddhava%E1%B9%83sa" title="Buddhavaá¹ƒsa">Buddhavaá¹ƒsa</a></i> (c. 1st and 2nd century BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main parts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C5%ABtra" title="SÅ«tra">Sutta Pitaka</a> and some portions of the <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">Vinaya</a> show considerable overlap in content with the <a href="/wiki/%C4%80gama_(Buddhism)" title="Ä€gama (Buddhism)">Agamas</a>, the parallel collections used by non-TheravÄda schools in India which are preserved in Chinese and partially in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Tibetan" title="Classical Tibetan">Tibetan</a>, as well as the various non-TheravÄda Vinayas. On this basis, these <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_texts" title="Early Buddhist texts">Early Buddhist texts</a> (i.e. the Nikayas and parts of the Vinaya) are generally believed to be some of the oldest and most authoritative sources on the doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Pre-sectarian_Buddhism" title="Pre-sectarian Buddhism">pre-sectarian Buddhism</a> by modern scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the material in the earlier portions is not specifically "TheravÄdan", but the collection of teachings that this school's adherents preserved from the early, non-sectarian body of teachings. According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Harvey_(Buddhism)" title="Peter Harvey (Buddhism)">Peter Harvey</a>, while the TheravÄdans may have added texts to their Tipiá¹­aka (such as the Abhidhamma texts and so on), they generally did not tamper with the earlier material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The historically later parts of the canon, mainly the Abhidhamma and some parts of the Vinaya, contain some distinctive elements and teachings which are unique to the TheravÄda school and often differ from the Abhidharmas or Vinayas of other <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_schools" title="Early Buddhist schools">early Buddhist schools</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> For example, while the TheravÄda Vinaya contains a total of 227 monastic rules for <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">bhikkhus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a> Vinaya (used in <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">East Asian Buddhism</a>) has a total of 253 rules for bhikkhus (though the overall structure is the same).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> These differences arose from the systematization and historical development of doctrines and monasticism in the centuries after the death of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2000217_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2000217-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Abhidhamma-pitaka contains "a restatement of the doctrine of the Buddha in strictly formalized language." Its texts present a new method, the Abhidhamma method, which attempts to build a single consistent philosophical system (in contrast with the suttas, which present numerous teachings given by the Buddha to particular individuals according to their needs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2000288_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2000288-75">[74]</a></sup> Because the Abhidhamma focuses on analyzing the internal lived experience of beings and the intentional structure of consciousness, it has often been compared to a kind of <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenological</a> <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> by numerous modern scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Nyanaponika_Thera" title="Nyanaponika Thera">Nyanaponika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Bodhi" title="Bhikkhu Bodhi">Bhikkhu Bodhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Piatigorsky" title="Alexander Piatigorsky">Alexander Piatigorsky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The TheravÄda school has traditionally held the doctrinal position that the canonical Abhidhamma Pitaka was actually taught by the Buddha himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Modern scholarship in contrast, has generally held that the Abhidhamma texts date from the 3rd century BCE onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> However some scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Erich_Frauwallner" title="Erich Frauwallner">Frauwallner</a>, also hold that the early Abhidhamma texts developed out of <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegetical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catechesis" title="Catechesis">catechetical</a> work which made use of doctrinal lists which can be seen in the suttas, called <i>matikas.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-canonical_literature">Non-canonical literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Non-canonical literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pali_literature" title="Pali literature">Pali literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg/240px-Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg/360px-Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg/480px-Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhaghosa_with_three_copies_of_Visuddhimagga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a> (right) (c. 5th century), shown here presenting three copies of his influential doctrinal <a href="/wiki/Compendium" title="Compendium">compendium</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a></i>, to the elders of the Sri Lankan Mahavihara school.</div></div></div>
<p>There are numerous TheravÄda works which are important for the tradition even though they are not part of the Tipiá¹­aka. Perhaps the most important texts apart from the Tipiá¹­aka are the works of the influential scholar <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a> (4thâ€“5th century CE), known for his <a href="/wiki/Atthakatha" title="Atthakatha">PÄli commentaries</a> (which were based on older Sri Lankan commentaries of the Mahavihara tradition). He is also the author of a very important compendium of TheravÄda doctrine, the <i><a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosby,_2013,_p._86_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby,_2013,_p._86-81">[80]</a></sup> Other figures like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhammapala" title="Dhammapala">Dhammapala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhadatta" title="Buddhadatta">Buddhadatta</a> also wrote TheravÄda commentaries and other works in Pali during the time of Buddhaghosa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> While these texts do not have the same scriptural authority in TheravÄda as the Tipiá¹­aka, they remain influential works for the <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a> of the Tipiá¹­aka.
</p><p>An important genre of TheravÄdin literature is shorter handbooks and summaries, which serve as introductions and study guides for the larger commentaries. Two of the more influential summaries are Sariputta Thera's <i>PÄlimuttakavinayavinicchayasaá¹…gaha,</i> a summary of Buddhaghosa's Vinaya commentary and Anuruddha's <i><a href="/wiki/Abhidhammattha-sangaha" title="Abhidhammattha-sangaha">Abhidhammaá¹­á¹­hasaá¹…gaha</a></i> (a "Manual of Abhidhamma").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosby,_2013,_86_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby,_2013,_86-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the history of TheravÄda, TheravÄda monks also produced other works of PÄli literature such as historical chronicles (like the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipavamsa" title="Dipavamsa">Dipavamsa</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavamsa" title="Mahavamsa">Mahavamsa</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographies</a>, poetry, PÄli grammars, and "<a href="/wiki/Sub-commentaries_(Therav%C4%81da)" title="Sub-commentaries (TheravÄda)">sub-commentaries</a>" (that is, commentaries on the commentaries).
</p><p>While PÄli texts are symbolically and ritually important for many TheravÄdins, most people are likely to access Buddhist teachings through vernacular literature, oral teachings, sermons, art and performance as well as films and Internet media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> According to Kate Crosby, "there is a far greater volume of TheravÄda literature in vernacular languages than in PÄli."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important genre of TheravÄdin literature, in both PÄli and vernacular languages, are the <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jataka tales</a>, stories of the Buddha's past lives. They are very popular among all classes and are rendered in a wide variety of media formats, from cartoons to high literature. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vessantara_Jataka" title="Vessantara Jataka">Vessantara JÄtaka</a> is one of the most popular of these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most TheravÄda Buddhists generally consider MahÄyÄna Buddhist scriptures to be <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">apocryphal</a>, meaning that they are not authentic words of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine">Doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="475" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg/240px-Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg/360px-Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sermon_in_the_Deer_Park_depicted_at_Wat_Chedi_Liem-KayEss-1.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of Buddha's first sermon from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wat_Chedi_Liem" title="Wat Chedi Liem">Wat Chedi Liem</a> in Thailand</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_teachings">Core teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Core teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The core of TheravÄda Buddhist doctrine is contained in the PÄli Canon, the only complete collection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_Texts" title="Early Buddhist Texts">Early Buddhist Texts</a> surviving in a classical <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indic language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> These basic Buddhist ideas are shared by the other Early Buddhist schools <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_Points_Unifying_the_Therav%C4%81da_and_the_Mah%C4%81y%C4%81na" title="Basic Points Unifying the TheravÄda and the MahÄyÄna">as well as by Mahayana traditions</a>. They include central concepts such as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>A doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Buddhism" title="Karma in Buddhism">Karma</a> (action), which is based on intention (<i><a href="/wiki/Cetan%C4%81" title="CetanÄ">cetana</a></i>) and a related doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Rebirth_(Buddhism)" title="Rebirth (Buddhism)">rebirth</a> which holds that after death, sentient beings which are not fully awakened will transmigrate to another body, possibly in another <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_cosmology" title="Buddhist cosmology">realm of existence</a>. The type of realm one will be reborn in is determined by the being's past karma. This cyclical universe filled with birth and death is named <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saá¹ƒsÄra">samsara</a>.</li>
<li>A rejection of other doctrines and practices found in <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Brahmanical Hinduism</a>, including the idea that the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> are a divine authority. Any form of sacrifices to the gods (including <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice_in_Hinduism" title="Animal sacrifice in Hinduism">animal sacrifices</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a> by bathing are considered useless and spiritually corrupted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> The PÄli texts also reject the idea that <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_India" title="Caste system in India">castes</a> are divinely ordained.</li>
<li>A set of major teachings called the <i><a href="/wiki/Bodhipakkhiy%C4%81dhamm%C4%81" title="BodhipakkhiyÄdhammÄ">bodhipakkhiyÄdhammÄ</a></i> (factors conducive to awakening).</li>
<li>Descriptions of various meditative practices or states, namely the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="DhyÄna in Buddhism"><i>jhanas</i></a> (meditative absorptions) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="DhyÄna in Buddhism">the formless dimensions (<i>arupÄyatana</i>).</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhist_ethics" title="Buddhist ethics">Ethical training (<i>sila</i>)</a> including the ten courses of wholesome action and the <a href="/wiki/Five_precepts" title="Five precepts">five precepts</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">Nirvana</a> (Pali: <i>nibbana</i>), the highest good and final goal in TheravÄda Buddhism. It is the complete and final end of suffering, a state of perfection. It is also the end of all rebirth, but it is <i>not</i> an annihilation (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ucchedavada" title="Ucchedavada">uccheda</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>The corruptions or influxes (<i><a href="/wiki/Asava" title="Asava">Äsavas</a></i>), such as the corruption of sensual pleasures (<i>kÄmÄsava</i>), existence-corruption (<i>bhavÄsava</i>), and ignorance-corruption (<i>avijjÄsava</i>).</li>
<li>The doctrine of impermanence (<i><a href="/wiki/Impermanence" title="Impermanence">anicca</a></i>), which holds that all physical and mental phenomena are transient, unstable and inconstant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DavidsStede1921p355_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsStede1921p355-92">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li>The doctrine of not-self (<i><a href="/wiki/Anatt%C4%81" title="AnattÄ">anatta</a></i>), which holds that all the constituents of a person, namely, the <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">five aggregates</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rupa_(Buddhism)" title="Rupa (Buddhism)">physical form</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vedan%C4%81" title="VedanÄ">feelings</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83j%C3%B1%C4%81" title="Saá¹ƒjÃ±Ä">perceptions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%85kh%C4%81ra" title="Saá¹…khÄra">intentions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vij%C3%B1%C4%81na" title="VijÃ±Äna">consciousness</a>), are empty of a self (<i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ä€tman (Hinduism)">atta</a></i>), since they are impermanent and not always under our control. Therefore, there is no unchanging substance, permanent self, <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>, or essence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Five_hindrances" title="Five hindrances">Five hindrances</a> (<i>paÃ±ca nÄ«varaá¹‡Äni</i>), which are obstacles to meditation: (1) sense desire, (2) hostility, (3) sloth and torpor, (4) restlessness and worry and (5) doubt.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Brahmavihara" title="Brahmavihara">Four Divine Abodes</a> (<i>brahmavihÄrÄ</i>), also known as the four immeasurables (<i>appamaÃ±Ã±Ä</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">The Four Noble Truths</a>, which state, in brief: (1) There is <a href="/wiki/Du%E1%B8%A5kha" title="Duá¸¥kha"><i>dukkha</i></a> (suffering, unease); (2) There is a cause of dukkha, mainly craving (<i><a href="/wiki/Ta%E1%B9%87h%C4%81" title="Taá¹‡hÄ">tanha</a></i>); (3) The removal of craving leads to the end (<i><a href="/wiki/Nirodha" title="Nirodha">nirodha</a></i>) of suffering, and (4) there is a path (<i>magga</i>) to follow to bring this about.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>The framework of <a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="PratÄ«tyasamutpÄda">Dependent Arising</a> (<i>paá¹­iccasamuppÄda</i>), which explains how suffering arises (beginning with <a href="/wiki/Avidy%C4%81_(Buddhism)" title="AvidyÄ (Buddhism)">ignorance</a> and ending in birth, old age and death) and how suffering can be brought to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Way" title="Middle Way">Middle Way</a>, which is seen as having two major facets. First, it is a middle path between extreme asceticism and sensual indulgence. It is also seen as a middle view between the idea that at death beings are annihilated and the idea that there is an eternal self (Pali: <i>atta</i>).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">The Noble Eightfold Path</a>, one of the main outlines of the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_paths_to_liberation" title="Buddhist paths to liberation">Buddhist path to awakening.</a> The eight factors are: Right View, Right Intention, Right Speech, Right Conduct, Right Livelihood, Right Effort, Right Mindfulness, and Right <a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">Samadhi</a>.</li>
<li>The practice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refuge_(Buddhism)" title="Refuge (Buddhism)">taking refuge in the "Triple Gems"</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharma_(Buddhism)" title="Dharma (Buddhism)">Dhamma</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sangha_(Buddhism)" title="Sangha (Buddhism)">Saá¹…gha</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Seven_Factors_of_Awakening" title="Seven Factors of Awakening">Seven Aids to Awakening</a> (<i>satta bojjhaá¹…gÄ</i>): mindfulness (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mindfulness_(Buddhism)" title="Mindfulness (Buddhism)">sati</a></i>), investigation (<i><a href="/wiki/Dhamma_vicaya" title="Dhamma vicaya">dhamma vicaya</a></i>), energy (<i><a href="/wiki/V%C4%ABrya" title="VÄ«rya">viriya</a></i>), bliss (<i><a href="/wiki/P%C4%ABti" title="PÄ«ti">pÄ«ti</a></i>)<i>,</i> relaxation (<i><a href="/wiki/Passaddhi" title="Passaddhi">passaddhi</a></i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%C4%81dhi_(Buddhism)" title="SamÄdhi (Buddhism)">samÄdhi</a>, and equanimity (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upekkha" title="Upekkha">upekkha</a></i>).</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/%C4%80yatana" title="Ä€yatana">six sense bases (<i>saá¸·Äyatana</i>)</a> and a corresponding theory of <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">Sense</a> impression (<a href="/wiki/Spar%C5%9Ba" title="SparÅ›a"><i>phassa</i></a>) and consciousness (<a href="/wiki/Vij%C3%B1%C4%81na" title="VijÃ±Äna"><i>viÃ±Ã±ana</i></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>Various frameworks for the practice of <a href="/wiki/Sati_(Buddhism)" title="Sati (Buddhism)">mindfulness</a> (<i>sati</i>), mainly, the four <a href="/wiki/Satipatthana" title="Satipatthana"><i>satipatthanas</i></a> (establishments of mindfulness) and the 16 elements of <a href="/wiki/%C4%80n%C4%81p%C4%81nasati_Sutta" title="Ä€nÄpÄnasati Sutta"><i>anapanasati</i></a> (mindfulness of breathing).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abhidhamma_philosophy">Abhidhamma philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Abhidhamma philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Therav%C4%81da_Abhidhamma" title="TheravÄda Abhidhamma">TheravÄda Abhidhamma</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1117" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg/220px-Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg/330px-Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg/440px-Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ledi_Sayadaw_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ledi_Sayadaw" title="Ledi Sayadaw">Ledi Sayadaw</a>, was one of the great Abhidhamma scholars of the 20th century as well as a teacher of meditation.</div></div></div>
<p>TheravÄda scholastics developed a systematic exposition of the Buddhist doctrine called the <a href="/wiki/Abhidharma" title="Abhidharma">Abhidhamma</a>. In the PÄli Nikayas, the Buddha teaches through an analytical method in which experience is explained using various conceptual groupings of physical and mental processes, which are called "dhammas". Examples of lists of dhammas taught by the Buddha include the twelve sense 'spheres' or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayatana" title="Ayatana">ayatanas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">five aggregates</a> or khanda and the eighteen elements of cognition or <a href="/wiki/Skandha#Eighteen_DhÄtus_and_Four_Paramatthas" title="Skandha">dhatus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>TheravÄda traditionally promotes itself as the <a href="/wiki/Vibhajyav%C4%81da" title="VibhajyavÄda"><i>VibhajjavÄda</i></a> "teaching of analysis" and as the heirs to the Buddha's analytical method. Expanding this model, TheravÄda Abhidhamma scholasticism concerned itself with analyzing "<a href="/wiki/Two_truths_doctrine" title="Two truths doctrine">ultimate truth</a>" (<i>paramattha-sacca</i>) which it sees as being composed of <i>all possible</i> dhammas and their relationships. The central theory of the Abhidhamma is thus known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhamma_theory" title="Dhamma theory">dhamma theory</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karunadasa_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karunadasa-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> "Dhamma" has been translated as "factors" (Collett Cox), "psychic characteristics" (Bronkhorst), "psycho-physical events" (Noa Ronkin) and  "phenomena" (<a href="/wiki/Nyanaponika_Thera" title="Nyanaponika Thera">Nyanaponika Thera</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosby,_Kate_2013_p._2_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby,_Kate_2013_p._2-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Sri Lankan scholar <a href="/wiki/Y._Karunadasa" title="Y. Karunadasa">Y. Karunadasa</a>, a dhammas ("principles" or "elements") are "those items that result when the process of analysis is taken to its ultimate limits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karunadasa_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karunadasa-99">[98]</a></sup> However, this does not mean that they have an independent existence, for it is "only for the purposes of description" that they are postulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karunadasa,_Y_p._14_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karunadasa,_Y_p._14-102">[101]</a></sup> Noa Ronkin defines dhammas as "the constituents of sentient experience; the irreducible 'building blocks' that make up one's world, albeit they are not static mental contents and certainly not substances."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> Thus, while in TheravÄda Abhidhamma, dhammas are the ultimate constituents of experience, they are not seen as <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essences</a> or independent particulars, since they are empty (<a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C5%ABnyat%C4%81" title="ÅšÅ«nyatÄ"><i>suÃ±Ã±a</i></a>) of a self (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ä€tman (Hinduism)"><i>attÄ</i></a>) and conditioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> This is spelled out in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patisambhidamagga" title="Patisambhidamagga"><i>Patisambhidhamagga</i></a>, which states that dhammas are empty of <a href="/wiki/Svabhava" title="Svabhava">svabhava</a> (<i>sabhavena suÃ±Ã±am</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ronkin, the canonical PÄli Abhidhamma remains pragmatic and psychological, and "does not take much interest in <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a>" in contrast with the <a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">Sarvastivada</a> tradition. Paul Williams also notes that the Abhidhamma remains focused on the practicalities of insight meditation and leaves ontology "relatively unexplored".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Ronkin does note however that later TheravÄda sub-commentaries (<i>á¹­Ä«kÄ</i>) do show a doctrinal shift towards ontological <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">realism</a> from the earlier epistemic and practical concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, Y. Karunadasa contends that the tradition of realism goes back to the earliest discourses, as opposed to developing only in later Theravada sub-commentaries:
</p>
<blockquote><p>If we base ourselves on the Pali Nikayas, then we should be compelled to conclude that Buddhism is realistic. There is no explicit denial anywhere of the external world. Nor is there any positive evidence to show that the world is mind-made or simply a projection of subjective thoughts. That Buddhism recognizes the extra-mental existence of matter and, the external world is clearly suggested by the texts. Throughout the discourses it is the language of realism that one encounters. The whole Buddhist practical doctrine and discipline, which has the attainment of Nibbana as its final goal, is based on the recognition of the material world and the conscious living beings living therein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The TheravÄda Abhidhamma holds that there is a total of 82 possible types of dhammas, 81 of these are conditioned (<i><a href="/wiki/Sankhata" title="Sankhata">sankhata</a></i>), while one is unconditioned, which is <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)"><i>nibbana</i></a>. The 81 conditioned dhammas are divided into three broad categories: consciousness (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citta" title="Citta">citta</a></i>), associated mentality (<a href="/wiki/Mental_factors_(Buddhism)" title="Mental factors (Buddhism)"><i>cetasika</i></a>) and materiality, or physical phenomena (<i>rupa</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-109">[108]</a></sup> Since no dhamma exists independently, every single dhamma of consciousness, known as a <i>citta</i>, arises associated (<i>sampayutta</i>) with at least seven <a href="/wiki/Mental_factors_(Buddhism)" title="Mental factors (Buddhism)">mental factors (<i>cetasikas</i>)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-110">[109]</a></sup> In Abhidhamma, all awareness events are thus seen as being characterized by <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a> and never exist in isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-109">[108]</a></sup> Much of Abhidhamma philosophy deals with categorizing the different consciousnesses and their accompanying mental factors as well as their conditioned relationships (<i>paccaya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_(30583616922).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1857" data-file-width="4112" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_%2830583616922%29.jpg/240px-018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_%2830583616922%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_%2830583616922%29.jpg/360px-018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_%2830583616922%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_%2830583616922%29.jpg/480px-018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_%2830583616922%29.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:018_Sakka_in_Tavatimsa_Heaven_(30583616922).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sakka in Tavatimsa Heaven, Wat Yang Thong, <a href="/wiki/Songkhla" title="Songkhla">Songkhla</a>, Thailand.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ngaye_(Naraka)_in_Burmese_art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1526" data-file-width="2185" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ngaye_%28Naraka%29_in_Burmese_art.jpg/240px-Ngaye_%28Naraka%29_in_Burmese_art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ngaye_%28Naraka%29_in_Burmese_art.jpg/360px-Ngaye_%28Naraka%29_in_Burmese_art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ngaye_%28Naraka%29_in_Burmese_art.jpg/480px-Ngaye_%28Naraka%29_in_Burmese_art.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ngaye_(Naraka)_in_Burmese_art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Burmese depiction of a hell scene</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_cosmology_of_the_Theravada_school" title="Buddhist cosmology of the Theravada school">Buddhist cosmology of the Theravada school</a></div>
<p>The PÄli Tipiá¹­aka outlines a hierarchical cosmological system with various <a href="/wiki/Plane_(esotericism)" title="Plane (esotericism)">planes existence</a> (<i>bhava</i>) into which sentient beings may be reborn depending on their past actions.
