Good actions lead one to the higher realms, bad actions lead to the lower realms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1t_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1t-112">[111]</a></sup> However, even for the gods (<i>devas</i>) in the higher realms like <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>, there is still death, loss and suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main categories of the planes of existence are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1t_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1t-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><b>ArÅ«pa-bhava</b></i>, the formless or incorporeal plane. These are associated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="DhyÄna in Buddhism">four formless meditations</a>, that is: infinite space, infinite consciousness, infinite nothingness and neither perception nor non-perception. Beings in these realms live extremely long lives (thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalpa_(aeon)" title="Kalpa (aeon)">kappas</a>).</li>
<li><i><b>KÄma-bhava</b></i>, the plane of desires. This includes numerous realms of existence such as: various <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hells</a> (<a href="/wiki/Naraka_(Buddhism)" title="Naraka (Buddhism)"><i>niraya</i></a>) which are devoid of happiness, the realms of animals, the hungry ghosts (<a href="/wiki/Preta" title="Preta"><i>peta</i></a>), the realm of humans, and various <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">heaven</a> realms where the <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Buddhism)" title="Deva (Buddhism)">devas</a> live (such as <a href="/wiki/Tr%C4%81yastri%E1%B9%83%C5%9Ba" title="TrÄyastriá¹ƒÅ›a">Tavatimsa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tushita" title="Tushita">Tusita</a>).</li>
<li><i><b>RÅ«pa-bhava</b></i>, the plane of form. The realms in this plane are associated with the four meditative absorptions (<i>jhanas</i>) and those who attain these meditations are reborn in these divine realms.</li></ul>
<p>These various planes of existence can be found in countless world systems (<i>loka-dhatu</i>), which are born, expand, contract and are destroyed in a cyclical nature across vast expanses of time (measures in kappas). This cosmology is similar to other ancient Indian systems, such as the <a href="/wiki/Jain_cosmology" title="Jain cosmology">Jain cosmology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1t_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1t-112">[111]</a></sup> This entire cyclical multiverse of constant birth and death is called <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saá¹ƒsÄra">samsara</a>. Outside of this system of samsara is <i><a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">nibbana</a></i> (lit. "vanishing, quenching, blowing out"), a deathless (<i>amata</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a> reality, which is a total and final release (<i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">vimutti</a></i>) from all suffering (<i>dukkha</i>) and rebirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soteriology_and_Buddhology">Soteriology and Buddhology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Soteriology and Buddhology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to TheravÄda doctrine, release from suffering (i.e. <i>nibbana</i>) is attained in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_stages_of_enlightenment" title="Four stages of enlightenment">four stages</a> of awakening (<i><a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism" title="Enlightenment in Buddhism">bodhi</a></i>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[web 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supramundane_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-supramundane-116">[web 3]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sotapanna" title="Sotapanna">Stream-Enterers</a></i>: Those who have destroyed the first three <a href="/wiki/Fetter_(Buddhism)" title="Fetter (Buddhism)">fetters</a> (the false <a href="/wiki/View_(Buddhism)" title="View (Buddhism)">view</a> of self, doubt/indecision, and clinging to ethics and vows);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[web 4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[web 5]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sakadagami" title="Sakadagami">Once-Returners</a></i>: Those who have destroyed the first three fetters and have weakened the fetters of desire and ill-will;</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anagami" title="Anagami">Non-Returners</a></i>: Those who have destroyed the five lower fetters, which bind beings to the world of the senses;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw_8_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_8-119">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arahants" title="Arahants">Arahants</a></i> (lit. "honorable" or "worthies"): Those who have realized <i><a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">Nibbana</a></i> and are free from all defilements. They have abandoned all ignorance, craving for existence, restlessness (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uddhacca" title="Uddhacca">uddhacca</a></i>) and subtle pride (<i><a href="/wiki/M%C4%81na" title="MÄna">mÄna</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw_8_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_8-119">[114]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>In TheravÄda Buddhism, a <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood" title="Buddhahood">Buddha</a> is a sentient being who has discovered the path out of samsara by themselves, has reached Nibbana and then makes the path available to others by teaching (known as "turning the wheel of the Dhamma"). A Buddha is also believed to have extraordinary powers and abilities (<a href="/wiki/Abhij%C3%B1%C4%81" title="AbhijÃ±Ä"><i>abhiÃ±Ã±Ä</i></a>), such as the ability to read minds and fly through the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The TheravÄda canon depicts <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a> as being the most recent Buddha in a line of previous Buddhas stretching back for aeons. They also mention the future Buddha, named <a href="/wiki/Maitreya" title="Maitreya">Metteya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[116]</a></sup> Traditionally, the TheravÄda school also rejects the idea that there can be numerous Buddhas active in the world at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha,_Burmese_manuscript.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="580" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha%2C_Burmese_manuscript.png/240px-Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha%2C_Burmese_manuscript.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha%2C_Burmese_manuscript.png/360px-Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha%2C_Burmese_manuscript.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha%2C_Burmese_manuscript.png/480px-Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha%2C_Burmese_manuscript.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sumedha_and_D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha,_Burmese_manuscript.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Burmese illustrated manuscript depicting Sumedha (the future Buddha Gautama) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%C4%ABpankara_Buddha" title="DÄ«pankara Buddha">DÄ«pankara</a> Buddha.</div></div></div>
<p>Regarding the question of how a sentient being becomes a Buddha, the TheravÄda school also includes a presentation of this path. Indeed, according to <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a>, there are three main soteriological paths: the path of the Buddhas (<i>buddhayÄna</i>); the way of the individual Buddhas (<i>paccekabuddhayÄna</i>); and the way of the disciples (<i>sÄvakayÄna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, unlike in Mahayana Buddhism, the TheravÄda holds that the Buddha path is not for everyone and that beings on the Buddha path (<a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva"><i>bodhisattas</i></a>) are quite rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[119]</a></sup> While in Mahayana, bodhisattas refers to beings who have developed the <a href="/wiki/Bodhicitta" title="Bodhicitta">wish to become Buddhas</a>, TheravÄda (like other early Buddhist schools), defines a bodhisatta as someone who has made a resolution (<i>abhinÄ«hÄra</i>) to become a Buddha in front of a living Buddha, and has also received a confirmation from that Buddha that they will reach Buddhahood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-125">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dhammap%C4%81la" title="DhammapÄla">Dhammapala</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cariyapitaka" title="Cariyapitaka">CariyÄpiá¹­aka</a></i> is a TheravÄda text which focuses on the path of the Buddhas, while the <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales"><i>NidÄnakathÄ</i></a> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhavamsa" title="Buddhavamsa">Buddhavaá¹ƒsa</a></i> are also TheravÄda texts which discuss the Buddha path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-125">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_doctrinal_differences_with_other_Buddhist_traditions">Main doctrinal differences with other Buddhist traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Main doctrinal differences with other Buddhist traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg/240px-Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg/360px-Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg/480px-Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moggallana%CC%9E-statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of the arahant <a href="/wiki/Maudgalyayana" title="Maudgalyayana">Moggallana</a>, who is identifiable by his dark (<i>nila</i>, i.e. blue/black) skin. He was one of the two most senior disciples of the Buddha and the foremost in psychic powers.</div></div></div>
<p>The orthodox standpoints of TheravÄda in comparison to other <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_schools" title="Early Buddhist schools">Buddhist schools</a> are presented in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kathavatthu" title="Kathavatthu">KathÄvatthu</a></i> ("Points of Controversy"), as well as in other works by later commentators like Buddhaghosa.
</p><p>Traditionally, the TheravÄda maintains the following key doctrinal positions, though not all TheravÄdins agree with the traditional point of view:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>On the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_time" title="Philosophy of time">philosophy of time</a>, the TheravÄda tradition follows <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_presentism" title="Philosophical presentism">philosophical presentism</a>, the view that only present moment phenomena (dhamma) exist, against the <a href="/wiki/Eternalism_(philosophy_of_time)" title="Eternalism (philosophy of time)">eternalist</a> view of the SarvÄstivÄdin tradition, which held that dhammas exist in all three times â€“ past, present, future.</li>
<li>The arahant is never a layperson, for they have abandoned the fetters of a layperson, including married life, using money, etc.</li>
<li>The power (<i>bala</i>) of a Buddha is unique and not common to the disciples (<i>savaka</i>) or arahants.</li>
<li>TheravÄda Abhidhamma holds that a single thought (<i>citta</i>) cannot last as long as a day.</li>
<li>TheravÄda Abhidhamma holds that insight into the four noble truths happens in one moment (<i>khaá¹‡a</i>), rather than gradually (<i>anupubba</i>), as was held by Sarvastivada. The defilements (<i><a href="/wiki/Kleshas_(Buddhism)" title="Kleshas (Buddhism)">kilesa</a></i>) are also abandoned in a single moment, not gradually.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Is there a source for this? I have listened to Theravada dhamma talks and as far as I can understand it certainly does not happen in an instant but is rather a gradual process (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>TheravÄda Abhidhamma traditionally rejects the view that there is an intermediate or transitional state (<a href="/wiki/Bardo" title="Bardo"><i>antarabhÄva</i></a>) between rebirths, they hold that rebirth happens instantaneously (in one mind moment).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup> However, as has been noted by various modern scholars like <a href="/wiki/Bhante_Sujato" title="Bhante Sujato">Bhikkhu Sujato</a>, there are canonical passages which support the idea of an intermediate state (such as the <i>KutuhalasÄla Sutta</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0y_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0y-129">[124]</a></sup> Some TheravÄda scholars (such as <a href="/wiki/Balangoda_Ananda_Maitreya_Thero" title="Balangoda Ananda Maitreya Thero">Balangoda Ananda Maitreya</a>) have defended the idea of an intermediate state and it is also a very common belief among some monks and laypersons in the TheravÄda world (where it is commonly referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Gandharva" title="Gandharva">gandhabba</a></i> or <i>antarabhÄva</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li>TheravÄda also does not accept the Mahayana notion that there are two forms of nibbana, an inferior "localized" or "abiding" (<i>pratiá¹£á¹­hita</i>) nirvana and <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)#Apratiá¹£á¹­hita_nirvÄna" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">a non-abiding (<i>apratiá¹£á¹­hita</i>) nirvana</a>. Such a dual nirvana theory is absent in the suttas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup> According to the <i>KathÄvatthu</i>, there can be no divining line separating the unconditioned element and there is no superiority or inferiority in the unity of nibbana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li>TheravÄda exegetical works consider nibbana to be a real existent, instead of just a conceptual or nominal existent (<i><a href="/wiki/Praj%C3%B1aptiv%C4%81da" title="PrajÃ±aptivÄda">prajÃ±apti</a></i>) referring to the mere destruction (<i>khayamatta</i>) of the defilements or non-existence of the five aggregates, as was held by some in the <a href="/wiki/Sautr%C4%81ntika" title="SautrÄntika">Sautrantika</a> school for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup> In TheravÄda scholasticism, nibbana is defined as the cessation (<i>nirodha</i>) consisting in non-arising and exists separately from the mere destruction of passion, hatred and delusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>TheravÄda exegetical works, mental phenomena last for a very short moment or instant (<i>khaá¹‡a</i>), but physical phenomena do not.</li>
<li>TheravÄda holds that the Buddha resided in the human realm (<i>manussa-loka</i>). It rejects the <a href="/wiki/Docetism" title="Docetism">docetic view</a> found in Mahayana, which says that the Buddha's physical body was a mere manifestation, emanation or magical creation (<i>nirmÄá¹‡a</i>) of a transcendental being, and thus, that his birth and death a mere show and unreal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> Also, the TheravÄda school rejects the view that there are currently numerous Buddhas in all directions.</li>
<li>TheravÄda holds that there is a ground level of consciousness called the <a href="/wiki/Bhavanga" title="Bhavanga">bhavaá¹…ga</a>, which conditions the rebirth consciousness.</li>
<li>TheravÄda rejects the <a href="/wiki/Pudgalavada" title="Pudgalavada">Pudgalavada</a> doctrine of the <i>pudgala</i> ("person" or "personal entity") as being more than a conceptual designation imputed on the five aggregates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doniger1980p168_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doniger1980p168-136">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup></li>
<li>TheravÄda rejects the view of the <a href="/wiki/Lokottarav%C4%81da" title="LokottaravÄda">Lokottaravada</a> schools which held that the all acts done by the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> (including all speech, defecation and urination, etc.) were supramundane or transcendental (<i>lokuttara</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup> Also, for TheravÄda, a Buddha does not have the power to stop something that has arisen from ceasing, they cannot stop a being from getting old, sick or dying, and they cannot create a permanent thing (like a flower that doesn't die).</li>
<li>TheravÄda traditionally defends the idea that the Buddha himself taught the <i>Abhidhamma Pitaka</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup> This is now being questioned by some modern TheravÄdins in light of modern Buddhist studies scholarship.</li>
<li>TheravÄda, <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">nibbana</a> is the only unconstructed phenomenon (<i>asankhata-dhamma, asankhatadhatu).</i> Unlike in the <a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">SarvÄstivÄda</a> school, <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Akasha" title="Akasha">akasa</a></i>), is seen as a constructed dhamma in TheravÄda. Even the four noble truths are not unconstructed phenomena, neither is the domain of cessation (<i>nirodhasamapatti</i>). "Thatness" (<i><a href="/wiki/Tath%C4%81t%C4%81" title="TathÄtÄ">tathatÄ</a></i>) is also a constructed phenomenon. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Dhammasa%E1%B9%85ga%E1%B9%87%C4%AB" title="Dhammasaá¹…gaá¹‡Ä«">Dhammasangani</a></i>, nibbana, the unconstructed element, is 'without condition' (<i>appaccaya</i>) and is different from the five aggregates which are 'with condition' (<i>sappaccaya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[135]</a></sup></li>
<li>In TheravÄda, the <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisatta path</a> is suitable only for a few exceptional people (like Sakyamuni and Metteya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup> TheravÄda also defines a bodhisatta as someone who has made a vow in front of a living Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup></li>
<li>In TheravÄda, there is a physical sensory organ (<i>indriya</i>) that conditions the mental consciousness (<i>manovinÃ±Äna</i>) and is the material support for consciousness. Some later TheravÄda works like the <i>Visuddhimagga</i> locate this physical basis for consciousness at the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> (<i>hadaya-vatthu</i>), the Pali Canon itself is silent on this issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-143">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-144">[139]</a></sup> Some modern TheravÄda scholars propose alternative notions. For example, <a href="/wiki/Suwanda_H._J._Sugunasiri" title="Suwanda H. J. Sugunasiri">Suwanda H. J. Sugunasiri</a> proposes that the basis for consciousness is the entire physical organism, which he ties with the canonical concept of <i><a href="/wiki/J%C4%ABvitindriya" title="JÄ«vitindriya">jÄ«vitindriya</a></i> or life faculty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-143">[138]</a></sup> W. F. Jayasuriya meanwhile, argues that "<i>hadaya</i>" is not meant literally (it can also mean "essence", "core"), but refers to the entire <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> (including the brain), which is dependent on the heart and blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-144">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>TheravÄdins generally reject the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_sutras" title="Mahayana sutras">Mahayana sutras</a> as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhavacana" title="Buddhavacana">Buddhavacana</a></i> (word of the Buddha), and do not study or see these texts (or Mahayana doctrines) as reliable sources. They reject the view that the <i>Tipitaka</i> is incomplete or inferior (i.e. "<a href="/wiki/Hinayana" title="Hinayana">Hinayana</a>") and that Mahayana texts are somehow more advanced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-145">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>TheravÄdins traditionally believe that an awakened <a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">arahant</a> has an "incorruptible nature" and are thus morally perfect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2000283_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2000283-146">[141]</a></sup> They have no ignorance or doubts. According to TheravÄda doctrine, arahants (as well as the other three lesser ariyas: stream enterers etc.) cannot fall back or regress from their state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_developments">Modern developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Modern developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
The modern era saw new developments in TheravÄda scholarship due to the influence of Western thought. As Donald K. Swearer writes: </p><blockquote><p>Although monastic education is still grounded in the study of Buddhist texts, doctrine, and the Pali language, the curricula of monastic colleges and universities also reflect subject matter and disciplines associated with Western education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swearer,_Donald_K_p._1642_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swearer,_Donald_K_p._1642-148">[143]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernist</a> trends can be traced to figures like <a href="/wiki/Anagarika_Dharmapala" title="Anagarika Dharmapala">Anagarika Dhammapala</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Mongkut" title="Mongkut">Mongkut</a>, and the first prime minister of Burma <a href="/wiki/U_Nu" title="U Nu">U Nu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[144]</a></sup> They promoted a form of Buddhism that was compatible with <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> and science, and opposed to superstition and certain folk practices. <a href="/wiki/Walpola_Rahula_Thero" title="Walpola Rahula Thero">Walpola Rahula</a>'s, <i><a href="/wiki/What_the_Buddha_Taught" title="What the Buddha Taught">What the Buddha Taught</a></i> is seen by scholars as an introduction to modernist Buddhist thought and the book continues to be widely used in universities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swearer,_Donald_K_p._1642_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swearer,_Donald_K_p._1642-148">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another modern phenomenon is Buddhist philosophers who received an education in the West, such as <a href="/wiki/K._N._Jayatilleke" title="K. N. Jayatilleke">K. N. Jayatilleke</a> (a student of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Wittgenstein</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Hammalawa_Saddhatissa" title="Hammalawa Saddhatissa">Hammalawa Saddhatissa</a> (who received his Phd at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>), going on to write modern works on <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</a> (<i>Early Buddhist Theory of Knowledge</i>, 1963 and <i>Buddhist Ethics</i>, 1987 respectively).  <a href="/wiki/Henepola_Gunaratana" title="Henepola Gunaratana">Henepola Gunaratana</a> is another modern TheravÄda scholar who studied philosophy in the west (at the <a href="/wiki/American_University" title="American University">American University</a>). The modern encounter with Christian missionaries also led to new debates (such as the <a href="/wiki/Migettuwatte_Gunananda_Thera#Panadura_Debate_à¶´à·à¶±à¶¯à·”à¶»à·à·€à·à¶¯à¶º" title="Migettuwatte Gunananda Thera">Panadura debate</a>) and doctrinal works written in defense of Buddhism or attacking Christian ideas, such as Gunapala Dharmasiri's <i>A Buddhist critique of the Christian concept of God</i> (1988).
</p><p>There have also been several modern TheravÄda scholars which have taken a historical critical perspective on TheravÄda literature and doctrine, attempting to understand its historical development. Some of these figures, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Kalupahana" title="David Kalupahana">David Kalupahana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhadasa" title="Buddhadasa">Buddhadasa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bhante_Sujato" title="Bhante Sujato">Bhikkhu Sujato</a>, have criticized traditional TheravÄda commentators like Buddhaghosa for their doctrinal innovations which differ in significant ways from the early Buddhist texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern era also saw new Buddhist works on topics which pre-modern Buddhists avoided, such as socially <a href="/wiki/Engaged_Buddhism" title="Engaged Buddhism">engaged Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_economics" title="Buddhist economics">Buddhist economics</a>. Thinkers such as Buddhadasa, <a href="/wiki/Sulak_Sivaraksa" title="Sulak Sivaraksa">Sulak Sivaraksa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayudh_Payutto" title="Prayudh Payutto">Prayudh Payutto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Karunatilake" title="Neville Karunatilake">Neville Karunatilake</a> and Padmasiri de Silva have written on these topics. Modern scholarship in western languages by western Buddhist monks such as <a href="/wiki/Nyanatiloka" title="Nyanatiloka">Nyanatiloka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyanaponika_Thera" title="Nyanaponika Thera">Nyanaponika</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%91%C4%81%E1%B9%87amoli_Bhikkhu" title="Ã‘Äá¹‡amoli Bhikkhu">Nyanamoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Bodhi" title="Bhikkhu Bodhi">Bhikkhu Bodhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Analayo" title="Bhikkhu Analayo">Analayo</a> is another recent development in the TheravÄda world.
</p>
<h2><span id="Practice_.28pa.E1.B9.ADipatti.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Practice_(paá¹­ipatti)">Practice (<i>paá¹­ipatti</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Practice (paá¹­ipatti)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dharmachakra,_withprint_(en).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1463" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Dharmachakra%2C_withprint_%28en%29.svg/300px-Dharmachakra%2C_withprint_%28en%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Dharmachakra%2C_withprint_%28en%29.svg/450px-Dharmachakra%2C_withprint_%28en%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Dharmachakra%2C_withprint_%28en%29.svg/600px-Dharmachakra%2C_withprint_%28en%29.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dharmachakra,_withprint_(en).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Dharmachakra" title="Dharmachakra">Dhamma Wheel</a> with eight spokes usually symbolizes the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_basis">Textual basis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Textual basis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li_Canon" title="PÄli Canon">PÄli Canon</a>, the path (<i>magga</i>) or way (<i>patipada</i>) of Buddhist practice is <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_paths_to_liberation" title="Buddhist paths to liberation">described in various ways</a>, one of the most widely used frameworks in TheravÄda is the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Blessed One said, "Now what, monks, is the Noble Eightfold Path? Right view, right resolve, right speech, right action, right livelihood, right effort, right mindfulness, right concentration."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[web 6]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The Noble Eightfold Path can also be summarized as the Three Noble Disciplines of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%ABla" title="ÅšÄ«la">sÄ«la</a> (moral conduct or discipline), <a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">SamÄdhi</a> (meditation or concentration) and <a href="/wiki/Praj%C3%B1%C4%81_(Buddhism)" title="PrajÃ±Ä (Buddhism)">PaÃ±Ã±Ä</a> (understanding or wisdom).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[web 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[web 8]</a></sup>
</p><p>TheravÄda orthodoxy takes the <a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga#Seven_Stages_of_Purification" title="Visuddhimagga">seven stages of purification</a> as outlined in the <i><a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a></i> as the basic outline of the path to be followed. The <i><a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a></i>, a Sinhala TheravÄda doctrinal <i><a href="/wiki/Summa" title="Summa">summa</a></i> written in the fifth century by <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a>, became the orthodox account of the TheravÄda path to liberation in Sri Lanka after the 12th century and this influence spread to other TheravÄda nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosby,_2013,_p._86_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby,_2013,_p._86-81">[80]</a></sup> It gives the sequence of seven purifications, in three sections:
</p>
<ul><li>The first section (part 1) explains the rules of discipline, and the method for finding a correct temple to practice, or how to meet a <a href="/wiki/Kaly%C4%81%E1%B9%87a-mittat%C4%81" title="KalyÄá¹‡a-mittatÄ">good teacher</a>.</li>
<li>The second section (part 2) describes <a href="/wiki/Samatha" title="Samatha">Samatha</a> (calming) practice, object by object (see <a href="/wiki/Kamma%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na" title="Kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna">Kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna</a> for the list of the forty traditional objects). It mentions different stages of <a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">SamÄdhi</a>.</li>
<li>The third section (parts 3â€“7) is a description of the five <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">khandhas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayatana" title="Ayatana">ayatanas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="PratÄ«tyasamutpÄda">dependent origination</a>, and the practise of <a href="/wiki/Vipassan%C4%81" title="VipassanÄ">VipassanÄ</a> (insight) through the development of wisdom. It emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Vipassan%C4%81-%C3%B1%C4%81%E1%B9%87a" title="VipassanÄ-Ã±Äá¹‡a">different forms of knowledge</a> emerging because of the practice. This part shows a great analytical effort specific to <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</a>.</li></ul>
<p>This basic outline is based on the threefold discipline. The emphasis is on understanding the <a href="/wiki/Three_marks_of_existence" title="Three marks of existence">three marks of existence</a>, which removes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avijja" title="Avijja">ignorance</a>. Understanding destroys the ten <a href="/wiki/Fetter_(Buddhism)" title="Fetter (Buddhism)">fetters</a> and leads to <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">Nibbana</a>.
</p><p>TheravÄdins believe that every individual is personally responsible for achieving his or her own self-awakening and liberation, each being responsible for his or her own <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Buddhism" title="Karma in Buddhism">kamma</a> (actions and consequences). Applying knowledge acquired through direct experience and personal realization is more heavily emphasized than beliefs about the nature of reality as revealed by the Buddha.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_conduct">Moral conduct</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Moral conduct">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Giving (Dana) is an important Buddhist virtue. The community of monastics is seen as the most meritorious field of karmic fruitfulness.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhist_ethics" title="Buddhist ethics">SÄ«la</a>, meaning moral conduct, is mainly defined as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_speech" title="Right speech">right speech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path#Right_action" title="Noble Eightfold Path">right action</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_livelihood" title="Right livelihood">right livelihood</a>. It is primarily understood through the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Buddhism" title="Karma in Buddhism">kamma</a>. In TheravÄda, one's previous intentional actions strongly influence one's present experience. Whatever intended actions are carried out will have future consequences, whether in this life or subsequent lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup> Intention is central to the idea of kamma. Actions done with good intentions, even if they have bad results, will not have negative kammic consequences.
</p><p>Several sets of precepts or moral trainings (<i>sikkhÄpada</i>) guide right action. After taking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refuge_(Buddhism)" title="Refuge (Buddhism)">Refuge</a> in the Triple Gems, lay TheravÄdin Buddhists traditionally take the <a href="/wiki/Five_precepts" title="Five precepts">Five precepts</a> (whether for life or for a limited time) in the presence of Sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup> Laypeople also sometimes take an extended set of <a href="/wiki/Eight_precepts" title="Eight precepts">Eight precepts</a>, which includes chastity during sacred days of observance such as <a href="/wiki/Uposatha" title="Uposatha">Uposatha</a>.
</p><p>Performing good deeds is another important feature of TheravÄdin Buddhist ethics. Doing so is said to make "merit" (<a href="/wiki/Merit_(Buddhism)" title="Merit (Buddhism)">puÃ±Ã±a</a>), which results in a better rebirth. The "ten wholesome actions" is a common list of good deeds:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Generosity (DÄna); This typically involves providing monks with "the four requisites"; food, clothing, shelter, and medicine; however, giving to charity and the needy is also considered dÄna.</li>
<li>Moral conduct (SÄ«la); Keeping the five precepts and generally refraining from doing harm.</li>
<li>Meditation (BhÄvanÄ).</li>
<li>Dedication of merit; doing good deeds in the name of someone who has passed away or in the name of all sentient beings.</li>
<li>Rejoicing in merit of good deeds done by others, this is common in communal activities.</li>
<li>Rendering service to others; looking after others or needy.</li>
<li>Honoring others; showing appropriate deference, particularly to the Buddha, Dhamma and Sangha, and to seniors and parents. Usually done by placing the hands together in <a href="/wiki/A%C3%B1jali_Mudr%C4%81" title="AÃ±jali MudrÄ">AÃ±jali MudrÄ</a>, and sometimes bowing.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dharma_talk" title="Dharma talk">Preaching or sharing the Dhamma</a>; the gift of Dhamma is seen as a form of highest gift. (Dhammapada 354)</li>
<li>Listening to Dhamma</li>
<li>Having right view or SammÄdiá¹­á¹­hi; mainly the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Three_marks_of_existence" title="Three marks of existence">three marks of existence</a>.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation">Meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">Buddhist meditation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2045" data-file-width="3318" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg/300px-Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg/450px-Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg/600px-Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_Man_Meditating.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>TheravÄdin monks meditating in <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, India)</div></div></div>
<p>Meditation (PÄli: <i>BhÄvanÄ,</i> literally "causing to become" or cultivation) means the positive cultivation of one's mind.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">Forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>TheravÄda Buddhist meditation practice varies considerably in technique and objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup> Currently, there are also various traditions of TheravÄda meditation practice, such as the <a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">Burmese Vipassana tradition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Forest_Tradition" title="Thai Forest Tradition">Thai Forest Tradition</a>, the esoteric <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tantric_Theravada" title="Tantric Theravada">BorÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna</a></i> ('ancient practices'), the Burmese <a href="/wiki/Weizza" title="Weizza">Weikza</a> tradition, <a href="/wiki/Dhammakaya_meditation" title="Dhammakaya meditation">Dhammakaya meditation</a> and the Western <a href="/wiki/Insight_Meditation_Society" title="Insight Meditation Society">Insight Meditation</a> movement.
</p><p>TheravÄda Buddhist meditation practices or <a href="/wiki/Bhavana" title="Bhavana">Bhavana</a> (mental cultivation) are categorized into two broad categories: <a href="/wiki/Samatha" title="Samatha">Samatha</a> bhavana (calming), and <a href="/wiki/Vipassan%C4%81" title="VipassanÄ">VipassanÄ</a> bhavana (investigation, insight).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[web 9]</a></sup> Originally these referred to effects or qualities of meditation, but after the time of <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a>, they also referred to two distinct meditation types or paths (<i>yÄna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VajiranÄá¹‡a-Mahathera_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-VajiranÄá¹‡a-Mahathera-163">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supramundane_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-supramundane-116">[web 3]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Samatha" title="Samatha">Samatha</a></i> ("calm") consists of meditation techniques in which the mind is focused on a single object, thought, or gatha, leading to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%C4%81dhi" title="SamÄdhi">SamÄdhi</a>'</i>. In traditional TheravÄda it is considered to be the base for <i>vipassanÄ</i> ("insight"). In the TheravÄda-tradition, as early as the <a href="/wiki/Nik%C4%81ya" title="NikÄya">PÄli Nikayas</a>, the four <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="DhyÄna in Buddhism">jhÄnas</a></i> are regarded as a <i>samatha</i>-practice. The eighth and final step of the Eightfold Path, Right Samadhi, is often defined as the four jhanas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[155]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Nik%C4%81ya" title="NikÄya">PÄli Nikayas</a>, JhÄnas are described as preceding the awakening insight of the Buddha, which turned him into an awakened being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[web 10]</a></sup> Yet the interpretation of <i>jhana</i> as single-pointed concentration and calm may be a later re-interpretation in which the original aim of <i>jhana</i> was lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolak2011_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolak2011-166">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Vipassana</i> ("insight", "clear seeing") refers to practices that aim to develop an inner understanding or knowledge of the nature of phenomena (<i>dhammas</i>), especially the characteristics of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">dukkha</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">anatta</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anicca" title="Anicca">anicca</a></i>, which are seen as being universally applicable to all constructed phenomena (<i>sankhata-dhammas</i>). <i>Vipassana</i> is also described as insight into <a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="PratÄ«tyasamutpÄda">dependent origination</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">five aggregates</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayatana" title="Ayatana">sense spheres</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-:10-167">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VajiranÄá¹‡a-Mahathera_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-VajiranÄá¹‡a-Mahathera-163">[154]</a></sup> It is the primary focus of the modernist Burmese Vipassana movement. In western countries it is complemented with the <a href="/wiki/Brahmavihara" title="Brahmavihara">four divine abidings</a>, the development of loving-kindness and compassion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFronsdal1998_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFronsdal1998-168">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Vipassana</i> practice begins with the preparatory stage, the practice of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%ABla" title="ÅšÄ«la">sila</a></i>, morality, giving up worldly thoughts and desires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson201454â€“55_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson201454â€“55-170">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[161]</a></sup> The practitioner then engages in <i><a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">anapanasati</a></i>, mindfulness of breathing, which is described in the <a href="/wiki/Satipatthana_Sutta" title="Satipatthana Sutta">Satipatthana Sutta</a> as going into the forest and sitting beneath a tree and then simply to watch the breath. If the breath is long, to notice that the breath is long, if the breath is short, to notice that the breath is short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup> In the "New Burmese Method" the practitioner <a href="/wiki/Sati_(Buddhism)" title="Sati (Buddhism)">pays attention</a> to any arising mental or physical phenomenon, engaging in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitaka" title="Vitaka">vitaka</a>, noting or naming physical and mental phenomena ("breathing, breathing"), without engaging the phenomenon with conceptual thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Practical_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Practical-174">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[165]</a></sup> By noticing the arising of physical and mental phenomena the meditator becomes aware of how sense impressions arise from the contact between the senses and physical and mental phenomena,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Practical_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Practical-174">[164]</a></sup> as described in the five <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skandhas" title="Skandhas">skandhas</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pa%E1%B9%ADiccasamupp%C4%81da" title="Paá¹­iccasamuppÄda">paá¹­iccasamuppÄda</a></i>. The practitioner also becomes aware of the perpetual changes involved in breathing, and the arising and passing away of mindfulness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Art_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Art-176">[166]</a></sup> This noticing is accompanied by reflections on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pa%E1%B9%ADiccasamupp%C4%81da" title="Paá¹­iccasamuppÄda">causation</a> and other Buddhist teachings, leading to insight into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">dukkha</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">anatta</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anicca" title="Anicca">anicca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Art_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Art-176">[166]</a></sup> When the three characteristics have been comprehended, reflection subdues and the process of noticing accelerates, noting phenomena in general without necessarily naming them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyanaponika1998_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyanaponika1998-179">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997133_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997133-180">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to VajiranÄá¹‡a Mahathera, writing from a traditional and text-based point of view, in the PÄli Canon whether one begins the practice by way of samatha or by way of vipassanÄ is generally seen as depending on one's temperament. According to VajiranÄá¹‡a Mahathera, it is generally held that there are two kinds of individuals. Those of a passionate disposition (or those who enter the path by faith) attain Arahatship through vipassanÄ preceded by samatha. Those of a skeptical disposition (or those who enter by way of wisdom or the intellect) achieve it through samatha preceded by vipassanÄ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VajiranÄá¹‡a-Mahathera_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-VajiranÄá¹‡a-Mahathera-163">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aims_of_meditation">Aims of meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aims of meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1787" data-file-width="1177" decoding="async" height="380" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/ff/Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg/250px-Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/ff/Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg/375px-Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/ff/Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg/500px-Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Ajahn_Mun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ajahn Mun, a key figure in the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Forest_Tradition" title="Thai Forest Tradition">Thai Forest Tradition</a>, is widely considered to have been an Arahant in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[171]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, the ultimate goal of the practice is to achieve mundane and supramundane wisdom. Mundane wisdom is the insight in the <a href="/wiki/Three_marks_of_existence" title="Three marks of existence">three marks of existence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supramundane_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-supramundane-116">[web 3]</a></sup> The development of this insight leads to four supramundane paths and fruits, these experiences consist a direct apprehension of Nibbana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supramundane2_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-supramundane2-182">[web 11]</a></sup> Supramundane (<i>lokuttara)</i> wisdom refers to that which transcends the world of <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saá¹ƒsÄra">samsara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supramundane2_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-supramundane2-182">[web 11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">nibbana</a>, there are various reasons why traditional TheravÄda Buddhism advocates meditation, including a good <a href="/wiki/Rebirth_(Buddhism)" title="Rebirth (Buddhism)">rebirth</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%B9%9Addhi" title="á¹šddhi">supranormal powers</a>, combating fear and preventing danger. Recent modernist TheravÄdins have tended to focus on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Research_on_meditation" title="Research on meditation">psychological benefits</a> and psychological <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_development_and_sources">Historical development and sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Historical development and sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The practice of TheravÄda meditation can be traced back to the 5th century exegete <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a>, who systematized the classic TheravÄda meditation, dividing them into samatha and vipassana types and listing <a href="/wiki/Kamma%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na" title="Kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna">40 different forms</a> (known as "<i>kammaá¹­á¹­hÄnas</i>", "workplaces") in his magnum opus, the <a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[174]</a></sup> This text has remained central for the study and practice of TheravÄda meditation. Buddhaghosa's commentary on the <a href="/wiki/Satipatthana_Sutta" title="Satipatthana Sutta"><i>Satipatthana sutta</i></a> ("Bases of mindfulness discourse"), as well as the source text itself, are also another important source for meditation in this tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crosby2013_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-crosby2013-186">[175]</a></sup> Buddhaghosa's work drew heavily on the Pali suttas as well as the Pali <a href="/wiki/Abhidharma" title="Abhidharma">Abhidhamma</a>. Kate Crosby notes that Buddhaghosa's work also "explicitly refers to the contemporaneous existence of secret meditation manuals but not to their content."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crosby2013_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-crosby2013-186">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Regarding post Visuddhimagga TheravÄda meditation, according to Kate Crosby, </p><blockquote><p>In the period between the Visuddhimagga and the present, there have been numerous meditation texts, both manuals and descriptive treatises. Many of the texts found in manuscript collections relate to meditation, some on a single, simple subject such as the recollection of the qualities of the Buddha, others more complex. Little research has been done to assess their variety. One difficulty is that meditation manuals as such are often in a mixture of a classical language, that is, Pali, and a vernacular that may or may not be a currently used language. Also, actual manuals often contain prompts or reminders rather than an in-depth explanation. In recent years it has emerged that there is still extant a relatively high number of manuals and related texts pertaining to a system of meditation called â€“ among other things â€“ borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna or yogÄvacara. Its core text, the MÅ«la-kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna "original, fundamental or basic meditation practice," circulated under a number of different titles, or without a title, throughout the Taiâ€“Laoâ€“Khmer and Sri Lankan Buddhist worlds. Some versions of this text are simple lists of kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna and from that perspective look entirely in accord with the Visuddhimagga or Theravada Abhidhamma texts. Other versions contain extensive narratives, explanations of symbolism, and of the somatic locations involved in the practice that make it clear that we are dealing with techniques of practice not described in the Canon or Visuddhimagga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crosby2013_186-2"><a href="#cite_note-crosby2013-186">[175]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>According to Crosby, the esoteric borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna or yogÄvacara meditation tradition was the dominant form of meditation in the TheravÄda world during the 18th century, and may date as far back as the 16th century. Crosby notes that this tradition of meditation involved a rich collection of symbols, somatic methods and visualizations which included "the physical internalisation or manifestation of aspects of the Theravada path by incorporating them at points in the body between the nostril and navel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[176]</a></sup> In spite of the novel elements in this meditation tradition, close study of borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna texts reveals that they are closely connected to Theravada Abhidhamma and the works of Buddhaghosa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup> Modernist reforms which emphasized Pali Canon study, a shift in state support to other traditions and modern wars in Indochina led to this tradition's decline, and it now only survives in a few Cambodian and Thai temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3235" data-file-width="2347" decoding="async" height="345" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg/250px-Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg/375px-Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg/500px-Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mahasi_Sayadaw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mahasi_Sayadaw" title="Mahasi Sayadaw">Mahasi Sayadaw</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the 19th and 20th centuries, the TheravÄda world saw a modernist revival and reinvention of meditation practice, as exemplified by the Burmese Vipassana movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswell2004890_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswell2004890-190">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008189_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008189-191">[180]</a></sup> According to Buswell <i>vipassana,</i> "appears to have fallen out of practice" by the 10th century, due to the belief that Buddhism had degenerated, and that liberation was no longer attainable until the coming of <i>Maitreya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswell2004889_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswell2004889-192">[181]</a></sup> The practice was revived in Myanmar (Burma) in the 18th century by <a href="/wiki/Medawi" title="Medawi">Medawi</a> (1728â€“1816) and by later figures such as <a href="/wiki/Ledi_Sayadaw" title="Ledi Sayadaw">Ledi Sayadaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahasi_Sayadaw" title="Mahasi Sayadaw">MahÄsÄ« Sayadaw</a> during the 19th and 20th centuries. These Burmese figures re-invented <i>vipassana</i>-meditation and developed simplified meditation techniques, based on the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satipatthana_sutta" title="Satipatthana sutta">Satipatthana sutta</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a></i>, and other texts, emphasizing <i><a href="/wiki/Satipatthana" title="Satipatthana">satipatthana</a></i> and bare insight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswell2004890_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswell2004890-190">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008189_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008189-191">[180]</a></sup> These techniques were globally popularized by the <a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">Vipassana movement</a> in the second half of the 20th century.
</p><p>Similar revival movements developed in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, such as the Thai forest tradition and Dhammakaya meditation. These traditions are influenced by the older borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[182]</a></sup> Thailand and Cambodia also saw attempts to preserve and revive the ancient "borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna" tradition of meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[183]</a></sup> In Sri Lanka, the new Buddhist traditions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amarapura_Nikaya" title="Amarapura Nikaya">Amarapura</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramanna_Nikaya" title="Ramanna Nikaya">RÄmaÃ±Ã±a NikÄyas</a> developed their own meditation forms based on the Pali Suttas, the Visuddhimagga, and other manuals, while borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna mostly disappeared by the end of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though the <i>Vipassana movement</i> has popularised meditation both in traditional TheravÄda countries among the laity, and in western countries, "meditation plays a minor if not negligible role in the lives of the majority of TheravÄda monks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf1995242_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf1995242-196">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrosby2013147_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrosby2013147-197">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun2014_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Braun2014-3">[web 1]</a></sup> Meditation is especially popular laypersons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf1995_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf1995-198">[187]</a></sup> especially during special religious holidays or in their old age, when they have more free time to spend at the temple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrosby2013147_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrosby2013147-197">[186]</a></sup> Buddhist modernists tend to present Buddhism as rational and scientific, and this has also affected how Vipassana meditation has been taught and presented. This has led in some quarters to a playing down of older non-empirical elements of TheravÄda, associated with '<a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[188]</a></sup> Strains of older, traditional TheravÄda meditation known as "borÄn kammaá¹­á¹­hÄna" still exist, but this tradition has mostly been eclipsed by the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernist</a> meditation movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_practices">Other practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg/250px-Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg/375px-Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg/500px-Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walk_with_lighted_candles_in_hand_around_a_temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Circumambulation" title="Circumambulation">Circumambulation</a> around a temple or a stupa is also a common devotional practice.</div></div></div>
<p>Laypersons and monks also perform various types of religious practices daily or during <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_holidays" title="Buddhist holidays">Buddhist holidays</a>. One of these is keeping a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_shrine" title="Buddhist shrine">Buddhist shrine</a> with a picture or statue of the Buddha for devotional practice in one's home, mirroring the larger shrines at temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-201">[190]</a></sup> It is common to offer candles, incense, flowers and other objects to these shrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-201">[190]</a></sup> Gestures of respect are also done in front of Buddha images and shrines, mainly the respectful salutation with the hands (<i>aÃ±jalikamma</i>), and the five-limb <a href="/wiki/Prostration_(Buddhism)" title="Prostration (Buddhism)">prostration</a> (<i>paÃ±c'anga-vandana</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_201-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-201">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhist_chant" title="Buddhist chant">Buddhist forms of chanting</a> is also widely practiced by both monks and laypersons, who may recite famous phrases such as the taking of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refuge_(Buddhism)" title="Refuge (Buddhism)">refuge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Metta_Sutta" title="Metta Sutta">Metta Sutta</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangala_Sutta" title="Mangala Sutta">Mangala Sutta</a> in front of their shrine. Chanting may also be part of the practice of recollection (<a href="/wiki/Anussati" title="Anussati">anussati</a>), which refers to contemplating various topics such as the sublime qualities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Buddha" title="The Buddha">the Buddha</a>, Dhamma and Sangha or the <a href="/wiki/Upajjhatthana_Sutta" title="Upajjhatthana Sutta">five subjects for daily recollection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_201-3"><a href="#cite_note-:6-201">[190]</a></sup> This may be done as part of a daily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puja_(Buddhism)" title="Puja (Buddhism)">puja</a> ritual.
</p><p>Another important religious practice for the devout is the keeping of special religious holidays known as <a href="/wiki/Uposatha" title="Uposatha">Uposatha</a> which are based on a lunar calendar. Laypersons commonly take the eight precepts while visiting a temple or monastery and commit to focusing on Buddhist practice for the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_201-4"><a href="#cite_note-:6-201">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Study (<i>ganthadhura</i>) of the Buddhist texts and listening to <a href="/wiki/Dharma_talk" title="Dharma talk">Dhamma talks</a> by monks or teachers are also important practices.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lay_and_monastic_life">Lay and monastic life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lay and monastic life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_monasticism" title="Buddhist monasticism">Buddhist monasticism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_monk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Young_monk.jpg/170px-Young_monk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Young_monk.jpg/255px-Young_monk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Young_monk.jpg/340px-Young_monk.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_monk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young Burmese monk</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinction_between_lay_and_monastic_life">Distinction between lay and monastic life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Distinction between lay and monastic life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, TheravÄda Buddhism has observed a distinction between the practices suitable for a <a href="/wiki/Householder_(Buddhism)" title="Householder (Buddhism)">lay person</a> and the practices undertaken by ordained <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">monks</a> (in ancient times, there was a separate body of practices for nuns). While the possibility of significant attainment by laymen is not entirely disregarded by the TheravÄda, it generally occupies a position of less prominence than in the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">MahÄyÄna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">VajrayÄna</a> traditions, with monastic life being hailed as a superior method of achieving <a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">Nirvana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[191]</a></sup> The view that TheravÄda, unlike other Buddhist schools, is primarily a monastic tradition has, however, been disputed.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Some Western scholars have erroneously tried to claim that MahÄyÄna is primarily a religion for laymen and TheravÄda is a primarily monastic religion. Both MahÄyÄna and TheravÄda have as their foundation strong monastic communities, which are almost identical in their regulations. Schools of MahÄyÄna Buddhism without monastic communities of fully ordained monks and nuns are relatively recent and atypical developments, usually based on cultural and historical considerations rather than differences in fundamental doctrine. Both MahÄyÄna and TheravÄda also provided a clear and important place for lay followers.</p><div class="templatequotecite">â€”â€‰<cite>Ron Epstein, "Clearing Up Some Misconceptions about Buddhism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[192]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>This distinction between ordained monks and laypeopleÂ â€“ as well as the distinction between those practices advocated by the PÄli Canon, and the folk religious elements embraced by many monksÂ â€“ have motivated some scholars to consider TheravÄda Buddhism to be composed of multiple separate traditions, overlapping though still distinct.  Most prominently, the anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Melford_Spiro" title="Melford Spiro">Melford Spiro</a> in his work <i>Buddhism and Society</i> separated Burmese TheravÄda into three groups: Apotropaic Buddhism (concerned with providing protection from evil spirits), Kammatic Buddhism (concerned with making <a href="/wiki/Merit_(Buddhism)" title="Merit (Buddhism)">merit</a> for a future birth), and Nibbanic Buddhism (concerned with attaining the liberation of <a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">Nibbana</a>, as described in the Tipitaka). He stresses that all three are firmly rooted in the PÄli Canon. These categories are not accepted by all scholars, and are usually considered non-exclusive by those who employ them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The role of lay people has traditionally been primarily occupied with activities that are commonly termed <i><a href="/wiki/Merit_(Buddhism)" title="Merit (Buddhism)">merit-making</a></i> (falling under Spiro's category of kammatic Buddhism). Merit-making activities include offering food and other basic necessities to monks, making donations to temples and monasteries, burning incense or lighting candles before images of the Buddha, chanting protective or scriptural verses from the <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a>, building roads and bridges, charity to the needy and providing drinking water to strangers along roadside. Some lay practitioners have always chosen to take a more active role in religious affairs, while still maintaining their lay status. Dedicated lay men and women sometimes act as trustees or custodians for their temples, taking part in the financial planning and management of the temple. Others may volunteer significant time in tending to the mundane needs of local monks (by cooking, cleaning, maintaining temple facilities, etc.). <a href="/wiki/Pariyatti" title="Pariyatti">Study of the PÄli scriptures</a> and the practice of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">meditation</a> are less common among the lay community in the past, though in the 20th century these areas have become more popular to the lay community, especially in Thailand.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg/220px-Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg/330px-Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg/440px-Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monk_on_pilgrimage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thai monks on <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> in their orange robes.</div></div></div>
<p>A number of senior monastics in the Thai Forest Tradition, including <a href="/wiki/Buddhadasa" title="Buddhadasa">Buddhadasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Maha_Bua" title="Ajahn Maha Bua">Ajahn Maha Bua</a>, Ajahn Plien Panyapatipo, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Pasanno" title="Ajahn Pasanno">Ajahn Pasanno</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajahn_Jayasaro" title="Ajahn Jayasaro">Ajahn Jayasaro</a>, have begun teaching meditation retreats outside of the monastery for lay disciples. <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Sumedho" title="Ajahn Sumedho">Ajahn Sumedho</a>, a disciple of <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Chah" title="Ajahn Chah">Ajahn Chah</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/Amaravati_Buddhist_Monastery" title="Amaravati Buddhist Monastery">Amaravati Buddhist Monastery</a> in Hertfordshire, which has a retreat center specifically for lay retreats. Sumedho extended this to Harnham in Northumberland as <a href="/wiki/Aruna_Ratanagiri" title="Aruna Ratanagiri">Aruna Ratanagiri</a> under the present guidance of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ajahn_Munindo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ajahn Munindo (page does not exist)">Ajahn Munindo</a>, another disciple of Ajahn Chah.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lay_devotee">Lay devotee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Lay devotee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesak_in_Uttaradit_(Thailand)_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="585" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Vesak_in_Uttaradit_%28Thailand%29_1.jpg/220px-Vesak_in_Uttaradit_%28Thailand%29_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Vesak_in_Uttaradit_%28Thailand%29_1.jpg/330px-Vesak_in_Uttaradit_%28Thailand%29_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Vesak_in_Uttaradit_%28Thailand%29_1.jpg/440px-Vesak_in_Uttaradit_%28Thailand%29_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vesak_in_Uttaradit_(Thailand)_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ceremony walks with lighted candles in hand around a temple on <a href="/wiki/Vesak" title="Vesak">Vesakha</a> Puja in <a href="/wiki/Uttaradit" title="Uttaradit">Uttaradit</a>, Thailand.</div></div></div>
<p>In PÄli the word for a male lay devotee is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upasaka" title="Upasaka">Upasaka</a> and a female devotee is Upasika. One of the duties of the lay followers, as taught by the Buddha, is to look after the needs of the monk/nuns. They are to see that the monk/nuns do not suffer from lack of the four requisites: food, clothing, shelter and medicine. As neither monks nor nuns are allowed to have an occupation, they depend entirely on the laity for their sustenance. In return for this charity, they are expected to lead exemplary lives.
</p><p>In Myanmar and Thailand, the monastery was and is still regarded as a seat of learning. TheravÄdin monasteries have been providing free education to many children since ancient times. In fact, today about half of the primary schools in Thailand are located in monasteries. Religious rituals and ceremonies held in a monastery are always accompanied by social activities. In times of crisis, it is to the monks that people bring their problems for counsel and monks often took up the role of mediators in most disputes. Traditionally, a ranking monk will deliver a sermon <a href="/wiki/Uposatha" title="Uposatha">four times a month</a>: when the moon waxes and wanes and the day before the new and full moons. The laity also have a chance to learn meditation from the monks during these times.
</p><p>It is also possible for a lay disciple to become enlightened. As <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Bodhi" title="Bhikkhu Bodhi">Bhikkhu Bodhi</a> notes, "The Suttas and commentaries do record a few cases of lay disciples attaining the final goal of Nirvana. However, such disciples either attain Arahantship on the brink of death or enter the monastic order soon after their attainment. They do not continue to dwell at home as Arahant householders, for dwelling at home is incompatible with the state of one who has severed all craving."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the modern era, it is now common for lay disciples to practice meditation, attend lay meditation centers and even aim for awakening.
